    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_780013626" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/780013626" />
    <a title="Literary Ames {Against GR Censorship}" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship"><img alt="Literary Ames {Against GR Censorship}" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1297107033p2/2109885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/780013626?book_show_action=true">Dec 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Literary Ames {Against GR Censorship}" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Literary Ames {Against GR Censorship}" href="/user/show/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship">Literary Ames {Against GR Censorship}</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140435900" href="/book/show/80080.The_Golden_Ass">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=bought-in-2014">bought-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=1001">1001</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=dtb">dtb</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=laugh-out-loud">laugh-out-loud</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=male-authors">male-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2109885-literary-ames-against-gr-censorship?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer780013626" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12718483806456446448"><strong>Bestiality</strong>. Kidnapping. Mugging. Ye olde carjacking. Burglary. Assault. Murder. Female paedophiles. Incest. Male rape. Adultery. Animal cruelty. Serial killers in the making. Poisonings. <strong>Homosexual priest gangbangs</strong>. Shapeshifting. Gods and goddesses. The Seven Deadly Sins. Evil mother-in-laws. Drama. Comedy. Tragedy. Adventure. Romance. Horror. Urban legends. Stories within stories. Inspiration for that Hannibal episode where a person was sewn into a dead horse's belly.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1407157161ra/10648305.gif' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><strong>What doesn't The Golden As</strong></span>
  <span id="freeText12718483806456446448" style="display:none"><strong>Bestiality</strong>. Kidnapping. Mugging. Ye olde carjacking. Burglary. Assault. Murder. Female paedophiles. Incest. Male rape. Adultery. Animal cruelty. Serial killers in the making. Poisonings. <strong>Homosexual priest gangbangs</strong>. Shapeshifting. Gods and goddesses. The Seven Deadly Sins. Evil mother-in-laws. Drama. Comedy. Tragedy. Adventure. Romance. Horror. Urban legends. Stories within stories. Inspiration for that Hannibal episode where a person was sewn into a dead horse's belly.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1407157161ra/10648305.gif" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><strong>What doesn't The Golden Ass have?</strong><br><br>At this point I should probably be comparing <em>The Golden Ass</em> to the brutality shown in <em>Game of Thrones</em>, only this is much less about political maneuvering and Machiavellian plotting, but still, they're both not for the faint of heart. <em>The Golden Ass</em> is one of the first, or <em>the</em> first, human-to-animal transformation stories that run in the same vein as Disney's <em>Brother Bear</em> and <em>The Emperor's New Groove</em>.<br><br>With all of the beatings Lucius received as a helpless slave in donkey form, carrying loads too heavy for his four-legged form, having his fur set on fire, never allowed rest when he most needs it and forced to continue on or have his feet tied together to be hurled off a cliff - because that's what they did to lame animals - I feel like I need to donate to <a href="http://www.thedonkeysanctuary.org.uk/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Donkey Sanctuary</a>.<br><br>For a 1,900 year old novel, you realise that nothing's really changed in that time, socially speaking.<br><br>Sex scenes are surprisingly good. There's no hesitation. No repressed sexuality. No self-esteem issues. And all manner of positions are attempted. <br><br><blockquote>
  <i>'The only redeeming feature of this catastrophic transformation was that my natural endowment had grown too.'</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Typical man. Turned into a donkey and he's impressed with the increase in the size of his manhood.<br><br>Yelling 'FIRE!' when being burgled and in need of help:<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>'Then, leaving him there fatally crucified, he climbed to the roof of his hovel and shouted at the top of his voice to summon the neighbours; calling each one by name he gave out that his house had suddenly caught fire, reminding them that this involved the safety of them all. So everybody, frightened by the danger next door, came running in alarm to help.'</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Well, it's been proven. Video games don't make kids violent, a <em>lack</em> of video games does. Imagination is a dangerous thing. So many inventive ways to torture and kill, to humiliate and degrade. The devil makes work for idle hands, as they say. So parents, quickly stuff a Playstation controller into your little one's hands before they turn their minds to dastardly deeds.<br><br>Certain aspects of <em>The Golden Ass</em> really do get you thinking about contentious issues. <br><br>How do you define bestiality? Lucius is a man turned into a donkey. When it's proposed that he'll be allowed his choice of horses with which to procreate - is that bestiality? Is Lucius's fornication as an ass with a human woman bestiality? Does the fact that he has a human mind inside an animal body change the status of the sexual relationship?<br><br>Surprisingly, Apuleius doesn't deliver the stereotype paedophile. A lusty married woman sets her sights on her stepson. Oddly this is labelled incest though there appears to be no blood connection. And it's the same with rape. A cuckolded husband rapes his adulterous wife's toyboy lover as punishment. Perhaps male paedophiles and rapists were stereotypes even 2,000 years ago.<br><br>The feminist in me feels compelled to point out the unbalanced female representation. Many women were demonised as witches who pee on men's faces, who steal body parts from the dead, who are complicit in evil deeds, who are nymphomaniacs, adulterers, paedophiles, vain and jealous grudge-holding goddesses. Psyche (myth), Photis (Lucius's servant lover) and Byrrhena (Lucius's aunt) are the only exceptions. <br><br><a href="http://fineartarchives.com/william-adolphe-bouguereau-the-abduction-of-psyche/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1407157161i/10648306._SY540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><p>The Abduction of Psyche by William-Adolphe Bouguereau</p><br><br>During Lucius's journey, the stories he hears are mostly told at the dinner table, around the fire, as a distraction on a long journey, or as a comfort to distraught kidnap victims. Understandably storytelling was their main form of entertainment. Well, that and gossip, which was free or you provided a meal for the teller. I really enjoyed the mythical telling of Psyche and Cupid.<br><br>Each of the 11 'books' are self-contained chapters of about 20 pages with a spoiler-y summary of what's to come at the beginning, so it was easy to dip in and out. I wasn't particularly happy with the ending, in fact I skimmed and skipped around at that point. I can understand Lucius's gratefulness at the chance to become human again, and I'm aware of that ancient tradition of 'a life saved, is a life owed' [see Azeem of 1991's Robin Hood: Prince of Thieves], but I have an issue with blind faith. Lucius walks away from his previous life to devote himself and his future to worshipping his rescuer. That's just weird, from my 21st century non-religious perspective.<br><br>The translation very much played a part in my enjoyment of this ancient novel. I carefully researched which was right for me. I chose the Kenney edition as it seemed the least stilted of those available, and I'm glad I made that choice. <br><br>I never thought I'd enjoy a 2,000 year old novel, but I did. And you might, too.<br><br><a href="http://literaryames.wordpress.com/2014/05/30/library-search-produces-unexpected-lols/" title="Library Search Produces Unexpected LOLs" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">My funny mishap in trying to find a copy of The Golden Ass.</a><br><br><em>*Read as part of The Dead Writer's Society's Around the World challenge.</em></span>
  <a data-text-id="12718483806456446448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_53639392" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53639392" />
    <a title="Pierce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1808370-pierce"><img alt="Pierce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1243824964p2/1808370.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53639392?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pierce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pierce" href="/user/show/1808370-pierce">Pierce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140435900" href="/book/show/80080.The_Golden_Ass">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1808370-pierce?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53639392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1100836940065584256">This is the only Roman novel to survive in its entirety, and I for one wish more of them had made it. The Golden Ass is one of very few books I've read more than once.<br/><br/>Lucius (a character very loosely based on the author) is a young citizen growing up in a Roman provincial town in Greece. After completing his studies in Rome, Lucius decides to go on a trip through Boiotea, the region around Thebes. (Thebes was, in the ancient world, a literary punching bag that became almost synonymous with nefar</span>
  <span id="freeText1100836940065584256" style="display:none">This is the only Roman novel to survive in its entirety, and I for one wish more of them had made it. The Golden Ass is one of very few books I've read more than once.<br /><br />Lucius (a character very loosely based on the author) is a young citizen growing up in a Roman provincial town in Greece. After completing his studies in Rome, Lucius decides to go on a trip through Boiotea, the region around Thebes. (Thebes was, in the ancient world, a literary punching bag that became almost synonymous with nefarious magic and divine mischief, having been the setting for most of the weirder classical Athenian tragedies, such as the exploits of incestuous Oedipus and his messed up kids). <br /><br />Apuleius provides a uniquely Roman perspective on what had been traditionally Greek themes of divine retribution and magic by saturating his tale with the kind of X-rated sexual, fecal, and slapstick nonsense that Roman Senators and American teens seem to love. "Lucius'" sexual appetite and relentless curiosity find a way to change the young Roman citizen into a ne'er-do-well donkey whose travels become significantly more fun because of his new perspective. <br /><br />The tone of The Golden Ass is much like a report of findings and observations during an exploratory journey to a new world, but the majority of the book is composed of vignettes telling the stories of countless people meeting there demise under hilarious circumstances. The book can be loosely labeled a comedy of errors, but more accurately features several comedies with myriad errors that in some way affect a hapless donkey. How could that not be fun?<br /><br />The distribution of fun tales is very similar to but less structured than in the later Canterbury Tales. The difference is perhaps a product of TGA having be written in prose, but also because Apuleius performs the doubly-difficult duty of developing (whoa...alliterative much?) small tales capable of standing on their own while also starting, developing, and completing a much larger story and still having everything work together seamlessly. Maybe Chaucer felt he had to be fancy and write a poem instead because his pretense for sticking all his tales together was, when you think about it, kind of lame. ("I'm going to church. You are, too? Yay! Let's tell stories.")<br /><br />TGA is also a valuable first-hand (well, sort of first-hand) account of Imperial Roman provincial life. Close examinations will reveal a complicated system of Roman magistracies and gruff centurions interwoven with a much older and highly religious Greek culture that still features (ostensibly) "Athenian" trial courts and what seem to be a few localized monarchies. The author and his homonymic narrator often rely on common cultural features such as religion to provide a setting and explanations, and to move a very ambitious story along. However, Apuleius' Greco-Roman theology becomes confusing at times (wait...Cupid is Venus' lover? ew.) and for some reason Egyptian deities play a much more prominent role in Apuleius' story, and Apuleius presents them as being greater than and/or composed of all their Olympian counterparts. Yeah...I dunno.<br /><br />Disclaimer: The Golden Ass is not a children's book. I don't think the MPAA has a rating high enough for some of this stuff. But, hey, the classical world wasn't as easily offended by vulgarity. And, since we still read Aristophanes and Catullus today, clearly we aren't either, so just calm down and allow yourself to enjoy old literature. This book is worth the time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1100836940065584256" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

For a book from the second century A.D., it's a surprisingly modern novel, incorporating lots of great short stories better than the much later Canterbury Tales.</span>

<div id="review_201974799" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201974799" />
    <a title="Meg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5854828-meg"><img alt="Meg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339855287p2/5854828.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201974799?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meg" href="/user/show/5854828-meg">Meg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5854828-meg?shelf=ancient-texts-comedy">ancient-texts-comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201974799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer400922782434153143">This is one of my favorite books. I read it for a class on the origins of the cult of the Virgin Mary. As a graduate student in the History of Art, I was using this class to better understand the early Christian representations of the Virgin (pre 8th century). <br/><br/>The professor had placed it on reserve so I had to read it within the library. I never expected to be able to read it in one sitting, but once I started the book I just could not put it down. I had to move to a corner where there were no s</span>
  <span id="freeText400922782434153143" style="display:none">This is one of my favorite books. I read it for a class on the origins of the cult of the Virgin Mary. As a graduate student in the History of Art, I was using this class to better understand the early Christian representations of the Virgin (pre 8th century). <br /><br />The professor had placed it on reserve so I had to read it within the library. I never expected to be able to read it in one sitting, but once I started the book I just could not put it down. I had to move to a corner where there were no students because I could not help myself from laughing out loud quite frequently. No one prepared me for this delightful, sideways walk on the wild side of the Roman Mediterranean in the 2nd century.<br /><br />Apuleius was a devout worshiper of Isis. For the class, we were instructed to pay close attention to the attributes of Isis, since Mary would eventually take on these same abilities. After all, Isis was the Egyptian mother goddess whose son, Horus, died and was reborn (only his birth/death cycle happens every year -- corresponding with the seasonal flooding of the Nile, if I remember correctly), so it was only logical that Mary would become her in many ways. <br /><br />Jesus took on the many attributes associated with Mithra (his feast day being Dec. 25th for one), as well as Horus, Osiris and even a little bit from Apollo too. Mary's cult developed much later (somewhere in the 6th century). As Christianity spread across the globe, it was famous for learning about the local deities, and if they were not able to directly convert the population, the priests would in effect say "that god you are worshiping is just like saint so &amp; so, and if you pray to him to intercede for you to Jesus &amp; God the Father, your prayers will be answered". This type of absorption/conversion by taking a local deity and transforming it into a saint is responsible for why it is very difficult to trace the original roots of some of the early saints to an actual person. Yes, there were actual people who were martyrs, and some of them became saints that developed into cults, but there is a large group of early saints who have conflicting origin stories, and therefor many religious historians doubt they were actual people but were created to absorb, and transform the local deities into a saint to Christianize the area.<br /><br />At the time this book was written, the Isis cult was one of the major faiths, if not the most popular throughout the Mediterranean. In fact, as an art historian, The familiar mother &amp; infant poses of Mary and Jesus that were so popular during the Middle Ages, were direct copies of the poses used to depict Isis and Horus together.<br /><br />The professor also told us to notice Apuleius' treatment of the other popular religions of his day, but especially the degrading way he portrayed a female worshiper of Jesus Christ.<br /><br />Apuleius' raunchy romp is meant to be absurd, but also shows great truths of the Roman world, as well as prejudices and stereotypes from the perspective of a worshiper of Isis. This is why the ending is not out of joint from the rest of the book -- Lucius has struggled with his faith, has in essence gone through the trials of Job, and has prevailed and been rewarded.<br /><br />As others have mentioned, this book was known throughout the centuries to the well educated and clearly influenced The Canterbury Tales, The Decameron, some of Shakespeare's Comedies, and even Dante's Divine Comedy. The Golden Ass needs to return to the required reading list for a complete education. I believe that a critical reading of this book cannot help but expand the reader's mind and general world perspective; and because of all the farcical sexual encounters, the process will be a fun one. Sadly, this country's extreme conservative temperature will not tolerate returning this book to its rightful place of required reading until perhaps at the University level (and some would not even have it there, probably wishing to burn it -- especially for the way Apuleius portrays the Christian woman).<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="400922782434153143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374505325" href="/book/show/80099.The_Golden_Ass">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2580971-smiley?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2580971-smiley?shelf=classics">classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer354002404" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13399392415762151833">Reading "The Golden Ass" translated from Latin by Robert Graves is all right if you don't mind various episodes related to 'the God' or 'the Goddess', for instance, as mentioned in the last chapter. For instance:<br/><br/>"At length the Goddess advised me to return home. ... (p. 288)" or<br/><br/>... "The God added that under his divine care this man would achieve fame in a learned profession and that Asinius himself would be richly rewarded for his trouble." (p. 290)<br/><br/>It seems vague to me since, I think, it's writte</span>
  <span id="freeText13399392415762151833" style="display:none">Reading "The Golden Ass" translated from Latin by Robert Graves is all right if you don't mind various episodes related to 'the God' or 'the Goddess', for instance, as mentioned in the last chapter. For instance:<br /><br />"At length the Goddess advised me to return home. ... (p. 288)" or<br /><br />... "The God added that under his divine care this man would achieve fame in a learned profession and that Asinius himself would be richly rewarded for his trouble." (p. 290)<br /><br />It seems vague to me since, I think, it's written in the ancient times some 1800 years ago when there were no religions like we have nowadays. I can't help wondering how the hero conceive him/her, if it's a kind of revelation, whether they appear in his dreams, etc.<br /><br />The good point is that his fine translation is readable and enjoyable, therefore, we need to compare this version to some new ones published recently. I hope to find a few copies to read as soon as I can. Finally, I would like to invite my Goodreads friends to share your ideas on the newly-translated ones, thanks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13399392415762151833" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_481210395" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/481210395" />
    <a title="Tom" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1245181-tom"><img alt="Tom" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562256827p2/1245181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/481210395?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom" href="/user/show/1245181-tom">Tom</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140435900" href="/book/show/80080.The_Golden_Ass">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1245181-tom?shelf=roman-lit">roman-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1245181-tom?shelf=antiquity">antiquity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1245181-tom?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer481210395" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12777987644265651242">Of this book, Flaubert wrote, "If there's any artistic truth in the world, it's that this book is a masterpiece. It gives me vertigo and dazzles me. ... It smells of incense and urine. Bestiality is married to mysticism." Such literary cologne can be a bit jarring at times or just plain puzzling, but the overall effect is still quite seductive. One of the most entertaining stories I've ever read, though poor Lucious, the titular ass, suffers so many indiginities and whippings that the on occasio</span>
  <span id="freeText12777987644265651242" style="display:none">Of this book, Flaubert wrote, "If there's any artistic truth in the world, it's that this book is a masterpiece. It gives me vertigo and dazzles me. ... It smells of incense and urine. Bestiality is married to mysticism." Such literary cologne can be a bit jarring at times or just plain puzzling, but the overall effect is still quite seductive. One of the most entertaining stories I've ever read, though poor Lucious, the titular ass, suffers so many indiginities and whippings that the on occasion the whole thing threatens to collapse into slapstick (think of a sophomoric Ovid before he matured into a great writer). What it all adds up to in the end,I'm not sure. Something to do with the unpredictable and arbitrary nature of "Fortune," which is invoked often as either a mean-spirited or negligent or at best whimsical guardian. <br /><br />I read the Robert Graves translation, which seems fairly direct and mildly colloquial. Nothing particularly noteworthy in the language, but serves the madcap energy and drive of the narrative well enough. The NYRB review of new Sarah Ruden translation (see below), however, suggests that Graves either misses or sacrifices much of Apuleius's witty and complex wordplay and shifting tones for the sake of accessibility (maybe it was a rush job for balloon payment on his Majorca home?). I was planning on rereading GA at some point anyway, just for the sheer enjoyment of the story, but will probably do so much sooner now with the arrival of this exciting new translation that "slides felicitously across the vast range of styles in the original," according to reviewer.<br /><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nybooks.com/articles/archives/2012/dec/20/magical-donkey/?pagination=false">http://www.nybooks.com/articles/archi...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12777987644265651242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6164432-mel-bossa?shelf=0022-general-mythology">0022-general-mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6164432-mel-bossa?shelf=0008-fiction">0008-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2149817795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5667171537728309887">For its originality and salaciousness, The Golden Ass deserves three stars. <br/><br/>But while I enjoyed certain parts, I was disgusted with many others. I think I would have loved this maybe ten years ago. Back then I didn't mind misogyny, or couldn't even recognize it much I guess. I read a lot of white men who wrote about whores and witches and nymphos and all that stuff. I suppose I thought it was cool of me as a woman to have such an open mind and all. Now, these days I get put off by these juvenile</span>
  <span id="freeText5667171537728309887" style="display:none">For its originality and salaciousness, The Golden Ass deserves three stars. <br /><br />But while I enjoyed certain parts, I was disgusted with many others. I think I would have loved this maybe ten years ago. Back then I didn't mind misogyny, or couldn't even recognize it much I guess. I read a lot of white men who wrote about whores and witches and nymphos and all that stuff. I suppose I thought it was cool of me as a woman to have such an open mind and all. Now, these days I get put off by these juvenile stories of bestiality and women sorceresses finding ways to cuckold their stupid boring husbands. Then again, look, this was written two thousand years ago. I need to put it in perspective. And it was very well written and entertaining and in the end the beautiful, adoring portrayal of the Goddess Isis almost redeemed the whole gross book. <br />Some say it's a religious story. Some say it's a fable. Some say it's just a controversial novel. I don't know. I didn't read much into it. Wasn't that invested.<br />Of course being the Greek Antiquity freak I am, I loved the Cupid and Psyche tale and the many references to Greek mythology and Homer. <br />I seek beauty and grace and I shouldn't have read this book, really. It was kind of vapid and it reminded me of Ovid's the art of love. <br />Ugh. <br />Too much "grab them by the ***" attitude in both books...<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2172668239" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2172668239" />
    <a title="عماد العتيلي" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13946677"><img alt="عماد العتيلي" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563385366p2/13946677.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2172668239?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="عماد العتيلي" class="user" itemprop="url" name="عماد العتيلي" href="/user/show/13946677">عماد العتيلي</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to عماد by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/69169842-cath">Cath</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13946677?shelf=best-of-2017">best-of-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13946677?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13946677?shelf=2017-reads">2017-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2172668239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15330685414215279516">‎‫‏‬<img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495747007i/22852986._SX540_.jpg' width='420' height='142' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>‬‬<br/><br/><b>“Behold Lucius I am come, thy weeping and prayers hath mooved me to succour thee. I am she that is the naturall mother of all things, mistresse and governesse of all the Elements, the initiall progeny of worlds, chiefe of powers divine, Queene of heaven!”</b>.<br/><br/>• I’ve always loved the Classics. Mythology is my ancient true love.<br/>My friend Cath recommended this novel, and I feel truly grateful to her. I loved this novel, and while I was reading it I felt nostalgic and decided to read more mytholog</span>
  <span id="freeText15330685414215279516" style="display:none">‎‫‏‬<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495747007i/22852986._SX540_.jpg" width="420" height="142" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">‬‬<br><br><b>“Behold Lucius I am come, thy weeping and prayers hath mooved me to succour thee. I am she that is the naturall mother of all things, mistresse and governesse of all the Elements, the initiall progeny of worlds, chiefe of powers divine, Queene of heaven!”</b>.<br><br>• I’ve always loved the Classics. Mythology is my ancient true love.<br>My friend Cath recommended this novel, and I feel truly grateful to her. I loved this novel, and while I was reading it I felt nostalgic and decided to read more mythology in the coming months. <br><br>• The Golden Ass is the first novel ever written, and to my surprise, it dealt with the Metamorphosis theme (this proves how important it is).<br><br>• I enjoyed the language very much. Lindsay (the translator) did a great job. I felt like I was there looking at Lucius (the poor ass!) and experiencing his pain. Also, of all the stories told within the novel, I enjoyed reading the complete account of Psyche and Cupid love story the most (maybe it was my favorite part of the whole book!).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1510315864i/24410512._SY540_.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>• I highly recommend this novel to all of my dear Classics-loving friends.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15330685414215279516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RTUOMCR3VjJNpIoJ+TkTd/QAV6MEd/+kcTfSmNFmYm2CjGjSn3BZC+q0eEqUM3DHelsn1V9RnMbBRZ61ivElBQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hEne+QWZnlvn0F0bFFrCnk9Nn6M/3MQNLT+ofg/EJehD8Lgbvp6RYkDAr1h5UKEuwRbv1WT6p2+dTeRTVFNigA==')})
  }
//]]>

Hänen antisankarinsa riippuvat kynsin ja hampain kiinni elämässä – ja erityisesti kaikissa mahdollisissa sen tarjoamissa nautinnoissa.\n\nPetroniuksen Satyricon löytyy tästä teoksesta ensimmäistä The man was disfigured with wounds, but seemed to rouse himself a little as from a deep sleep, almost of death itself. Pain had clenched his eyes, but the sight of the maiden drew them toward her. He collected his breath, heaved a deep sigh, and murmured faintly. &quot;My sweet,&quot; said he, &quot;are you truly safe, or are you too a casualty of the war?&quot;\n\nThe Romance novel didn\'t begin with Kathleen Woodiwiss or even with the Bronte sisters. By the time Heliodorus wrote his &quot;Aethiopica&quot;--or &quot;Ethiopian Romance&quot;--in the third century, the genre was already impressively developed. Heliodorus launches his tale of love and the quirks of fate with a bizarre scene of blood, bodies, and booty on an Egyptian beach viewed through the eyes of a band of mystified pirates. The central love-struck characters are Charicles, the beautiful daughter of the Ethiopian queen, and Theagenes, a Thessalian aristocrat. The story unfolds with all the twists and devices any writer would employ today, with the added attractions of dreams, oracles, and exotic locales in the ancient Mediterranean and Africa.\n\nHadas\'s was the first modern English-language translation of this story, which was first translated into English in 1587 and was a favorite among the Elizabethans. His version of this earliest extant Greek novel remains accessible and appealing.&quot; -- back cover.\n\nThe novel is thought to have originally been written in the 2nd or 3rd century A.D. Seneca\'s The Apocolocyntosis, a malicious skit on the deification of Claudius the Clod\', was designed by the author to ingratiate himself with Nero, who was Claudius\' successor. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2499406009409654974">Apuleius (Lucius Apuleius Madaurensis “Africanus”; Berber: Afulay, c. 125 – c. 180 CE) was a Latin-language prose writer.<br/><br/>He was a Numidian Berber and lived under the Roman Empire. He was from Madaurus (now M'Daourouch, Algeria). He studied Platonist philosophy in Athens; travelled to Italy, Asia Minor and Egypt; and was an initiate in several cults or mysteries. The most famous incident in his lif</span>
  <span id="freeText2499406009409654974" style="display:none">Apuleius (Lucius Apuleius Madaurensis “Africanus”; Berber: Afulay, c. 125 – c. 180 CE) was a Latin-language prose writer.<br /><br />He was a Numidian Berber and lived under the Roman Empire. He was from Madaurus (now M'Daourouch, Algeria). He studied Platonist philosophy in Athens; travelled to Italy, Asia Minor and Egypt; and was an initiate in several cults or mysteries. The most famous incident in his life was when he was accused of using magic to gain the attentions (and fortune) of a wealthy widow. He declaimed and then distributed a witty tour de force in his own defense before the proconsul and a court of magistrates convened in Sabratha, near ancient Tripoli, Libya. (Wikipedia)</span>
  <a data-text-id="2499406009409654974" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1741202-metamorphoseon-libri-xi-asinus-aureus">Quotes from The Golden Ass</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I besprinkled their faces with my liquid dung and forced them to leave off.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/614966-i-besprinkled-their-faces-with-my-liquid-dung-and-forced">10 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Behold Lucius I am come, thy weeping and prayers hath mooved mee to succour thee. I am she that is the naturall mother of all things, mistresse and governesse of all the Elements, the initiall progeny of worlds, chiefe of powers divine, Queene of heaven! the principall of the Gods celestiall, the light of the goddesses: at my will the planets of the ayre, the wholesome winds of the Seas, and the silences of hell be diposed; my name, my divinity is adored throughout all the world in divers manners, in variable customes and in many names, for the Phrygians call me the mother of the Gods: the Athenians, Minerva: the Cyprians, Venus: the Candians, Diana: the Sicilians Proserpina: the Eleusians, Ceres: some Juno, other Bellona, other Hecate: and principally the Aethiopians which dwell in the Orient, and the Aegyptians which are excellent in all kind of ancient doctrine, and by their proper ceremonies accustome to worship mee, doe call mee Queene Isis. Behold I am come to take pitty of thy fortune and tribulation, behold I am present to favour and ayd thee, leave off thy weeping and lamentation, put away all thy sorrow, for behold the healthfull day which is ordained by my providence, therefore be ready to attend to my commandement. This day which shall come after this night, is dedicated to my service, by an eternall religion, my Priests and Ministers doe accustome after the tempests of the Sea, be ceased, to offer in my name a new ship as a first fruit of my Navigation.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1282856-behold-lucius-i-am-come-thy-weeping-and-prayers-hath">6 likes</a>
